10.1016/j.jmp.2025.102941
No data was used for the research described in the article.

Knowledge Structure Theory (KST), originally developed for dichotomous items by  Doignon and Falmagne (1985, 1999) , has since been extended to polytomous items. The foundational framework of polytomous knowledge structure theory began with  Schrepp (1997) ; since then, it has evolved significantly through the contributions of numerous researchers, including  Heller (2021)  and  Stefanutti, Anselmi, de Chiusole and Spoto (2020) , and more recently,  Stefanutti, Spoto, Anselmi, and de Chiusole (2023) .

These researchers have formalized the concepts of polytomous knowledge states and structures, expanded the fundamental notions of surmise relations and surmise functions from classical KST, and established specific Galois connections within polytomous structures. A key motivation for these extensions is to address the limitations of classical dichotomous KST, which assumes a strict binary division between mastery and non-mastery of items. By incorporating polytomous response models, researchers have introduced a more flexible and refined framework that enables knowledge states to distinguish multiple intermediate levels. This extension is particularly useful in educational assessments, where learners’ progress often follows a gradual trajectory rather than an abrupt transition between discrete states.

More recent developments have placed increasing emphasis on expanding the theoretical and methodological foundations of polytomous knowledge structures. One primary focus has been the generalization of surmise functions to the polytomous setting ( Wang, Li, Chen, Xu, & Xie, 2023; Wang, Li, & Sun, 2023a ), defining them on item-response level pairs and their clauses to better model different solution pathways across response levels. Additionally, significant theoretical advancements have been made in the establishment of comprehensive Galois connections ( Ge, 2022; Wang et al., 2023a ), which establish one-to-one correspondences between various surmise functions and certain polytomous knowledge structures. Researchers have also investigated well-gradedness properties in polytomous settings ( Sun, Li, He, Ge, & Lin, 2023; Wang & Li, 2024 ), ensuring smooth transitions between adjacent polytomous knowledge states to better reflect realistic learning processes. Another important development involves data-driven methodologies, including k-median clustering algorithms and probabilistic models designed to enhance the practical applicability of polytomous KST ( de Chiusole, Spoto, & Stefanutti, 2020; Stefanutti, de Chiusole, Anselmi and Spoto, 2020 ). These approaches enable the extraction of meaningful polytomous knowledge structures directly from response data, reducing reliance on predefined expert assumptions and making the framework more adaptable to diverse educational and psychological assessment contexts.

In addition to these advancements, competence-based knowledge structure theory (CbKST; e.g.,  Doignon, 1994; Düntsch & Gediga, 1995; Falmagne, Albert, Doble, Eppstein, & Hu, 2013; Gediga & Düntsch, 2002; Heller, Stefanutti, Anselmi, & Robusto, 2015; Korossy, 1997, 1999 ) has gained considerable traction, particularly through the use of skill maps or skill functions. These maps define the relationship between problems and the required skills, providing a structured framework for both knowledge representation and competence assessment. Different theoretical perspectives guide how these relationships are interpreted. In some models, problem-solving is assumed to require the combined mastery of all relevant skills, reflecting a cumulative learning process. In contrast, alternative approaches allow for more flexibility, recognizing that possessing any one of the necessary skills may be sufficient to solve a given problem. The choice between these perspectives influences how knowledge states are structured and how individual learning trajectories are assessed. As knowledge assessments increasingly adopt polytomous response formats, it becomes essential to extend these models beyond the dichotomous setting, enabling a more nuanced understanding of competence progression.

Building on this foundation,  Stefanutti et al. (2023)  expanded the concept of skills to encompass more general attributes, introducing polytomous attribute maps constrained by a set of conditions. They developed attribute structures, item–response functions, and constructed polytomous knowledge structures over more general partially ordered sets. As they noted, their attribute map is conjunctive in nature, meaning all assigned attributes must work together to reach an observable response level. However, in practical polytomous contexts, the conjunctive model is not always applicable, as attributes may sometimes function independently, each capable of enabling the item to reach a response level on its own.

For instance, consider solving quadratic equations of the form  
                         
                            m 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                            + 
                            n 
                            x 
                            + 
                            p 
                            = 
                            0 
                         
                      , where  
                         m 
                      ,  
                         n 
                      , and  
                         p 
                       are real numbers. A student who has mastered the factorization method — considered here as an attribute, denoted by  
                         a 
                       — may be able to solve simpler equations with integer or rational roots, thereby achieving only a partial score. Furthermore, a student who has mastered the quadratic formula (attribute  
                         b 
                      ) or the completing-the-square method (attribute  
                         c 
                      ) can typically solve any form of quadratic equation, thereby achieving full marks.

Hence, there is a need to investigate a different type of attribute map suited for such models. This paper introduces a new form of attribute map, constrained by four conditions to ensure its applicability. Consequently, the construction of attribute structures and item–response functions must follow these constraints, leading to an appropriate polytomous knowledge structure.

The paper is organized as follows: Section  2  introduces preliminary knowledge, Section  3  presents the main contributions, including the new attribute map and the corresponding item–response function, and Section  4  offers a mathematical example to illustrate the model’s applicability. Section  5  compares the proposed model with the existing conjunctive approach. Finally, Section  6  provides a concluding discussion.

In this section, we offer a concise introduction to the essential concepts of KST and its extension to polytomous contexts. For a more comprehensive treatment, we suggest referring to  Falmagne and Doignon (2011), Heller (2021), Stefanutti, Anselmi et al. (2020) , and  Stefanutti et al. (2023) , as well as  Heller and Stefanutti (2024) .

In KST, an individual’s  knowledge state  is defined as the set of problems

                         0̸

                         Q

                            S 
                         
                       an individual possesses. The collection of all possible competence states forms a  competence structure , which is required to include the empty set  
                         0̸ 
                       and the full set of skills  
                         S 
                      .

These two structures are closely linked through the use of a  skill map  and a  problem function . The skill map

Based on the concept of admissibility, they proposed a type of polytomous knowledge structure that may exist without a top element, referring to a specific case aimed at representing misconceptions, as outlined in  Stefanutti, de Chiusole, Gondan and Maurer (2020) . Moreover, in the dichotomous case, structures without a top element have been previously considered, with  Falmagne (2015)  introducing the notion of an assessment structure for such cases. Additionally, quasi-ordinal assessment spaces were characterized by  Heller (2019) .

Therefore, the following concept is largely adapted from  Stefanutti et al. (2023) . However, to maintain consistency with the dichotomous case, we introduce a slight modification and adopt the new term  polytomous assessment structure  instead of polytomous knowledge structure.

A polytomous assessment structure is required to include at least a minimal element. This minimal element serves as a baseline state, ensuring that the structure has a well-defined starting point. Moreover, the structure must cover the entire admissible relation  
                         R 
                      . This approach is distinct from frameworks such as  Heller (2021) , which are built upon item-specific response sets  
                         
                            
                               V 
                            
                            
                               q 
                            
                         
                       rather than a single admissibility relation over a global set of values  
                         V 
                      .

Stefanutti et al. (2023)  extended the concept of conjunctive skill maps in KST to the polytomous setting, where the term “skill” was replaced with the more neutral term “attribute”. To ensure formal rigor, they provided four conditions alongside the definition to properly define and constrain this extension. They named this newly extended mapping the attribute map; however, to distinguish it from the subsequent concepts, we will refer to it as the conjunctive attribute map in this paper.

                                        In (D3) and (D4),  
                                              
                                                 
                                                    F 
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    u 
                                                 
                                              
                                            denotes the set of common upper bounds of  
                                              F 
                                           . These conditions require the existence of at least one admissible upper bound  
                                              
                                                 w 
                                                 ∈ 
                                                 R 
                                                 
                                                    ( 
                                                    q 
                                                    ) 
                                                 
                                              
                                            satisfying the stated property, but not its uniqueness, as multiple such bounds may exist in a partially ordered set.

Condition (D1) indicates that for each  
                         
                            q 
                            ∈ 
                            Q 
                         
                      , the pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            q 
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  v 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       is not assigned any attributes. The interpretation of  
                         
                            
                               v 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       depends on the context, as explained by  Stefanutti et al. (2023)  in their discussion of Condition (C1). Critically,  
                         
                            
                               v 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       represents the lowest ideal response level: namely, the state in which none of the attributes required for higher responses are present. The following examples merely illustrate common coding practices and do not alter this theoretical meaning. In an educational assessment, for instance,  
                         
                            
                               v 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       might be assigned to a specific incorrect response that serves as a baseline (e.g., an answer that is formally wrong yet reveals no particular misconception). In a psychological or satisfaction survey, it could correspond to an explicit neutral or “not applicable” option, reflecting a deliberate choice by the respondent to indicate a lack of preference or relevant experience.

Condition (D2) states that for any  
                         
                            q 
                            ∈ 
                            Q 
                         
                       and  
                         
                            v 
                            ∈ 
                            R 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               ) 
                            
                            ∖ 
                            
                               { 
                               
                                  
                                     v 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                               } 
                            
                         
                      , the set  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       is nonempty. Furthermore, if  
                         
                            u 
                            ∈ 
                            R 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            v 
                            ≺ 
                            u 
                         
                      , then  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       must contain at least one attribute that is not present in  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               u 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      . Otherwise, if for every  
                         
                            a 
                            ∈ 
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       there exists some  
                         
                            u 
                            ∈ 
                            R 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       such that  
                         
                            v 
                            ≺ 
                            u 
                         
                       and  
                         
                            a 
                            ∈ 
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               u 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , then the attributes that distinguish the pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            q 
                            , 
                            v 
                            ) 
                         
                       are entirely subsumed by those associated with higher responses. Consequently, regardless of which attribute  
                         
                            a 
                            ∈ 
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       is selected, the resulting response value will always exceed  
                         v 
                      . Thus, the response  
                         
                            ( 
                            q 
                            , 
                            v 
                            ) 
                         
                       will never be observed in the final assessment.

Condition (D3) states that for a given item  
                         q 
                      , if  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               u 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       have a nonempty intersection, then there exists an admissible response level  
                         w 
                       that is not lower than  
                         
                            { 
                            u 
                            , 
                            v 
                            } 
                         
                       such that their intersection is a subset of  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               w 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      . This can be understood as certain attributes exhibiting a strong capacity, enabling them to address items at various admissible levels. For example, in a mathematics test, the attribute of “logical reasoning” might be applicable to both basic arithmetic problems (low level) and more complex algebraic problems (high level). Thus, this attribute would be present across different admissible levels of the test. Similarly, in a language proficiency test, the attribute of “comprehension” could apply to both simple sentence interpretation (low level) and complex text analysis (high level), indicating its presence across various levels.

Condition (D4) stipulates that if both  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               u 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       have nonempty intersections with  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               r 
                               , 
                               x 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , then there must exist an admissible level  
                         w 
                       that is not lower than  
                         
                            { 
                            u 
                            , 
                            v 
                            } 
                         
                       such that  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               w 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       also has a nonempty intersection with  
                         
                            μ 
                            
                               ( 
                               r 
                               , 
                               x 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      . The rationale behind this requirement is that if both  
                         
                            ( 
                            q 
                            , 
                            v 
                            ) 
                         
                       and  
                         
                            ( 
                            q 
                            , 
                            u 
                            ) 
                         
                       are observed, then under the disjunctive interpretation,  
                         
                            ( 
                            q 
                            , 
                            w 
                            ) 
                         
                       should likewise be observed.

Stefanutti et al. (2023)  introduced the concepts of compatibility and completeness, though these were applied in the context of constructing polytomous assessment structures with conjunctive attribute maps. In this paper, we also require analogous concepts of compatibility and completeness, but these are defined in a disjunctive context.

Having established the definition of a disjunctive attribute map, we have also confirmed the existence of a (maximal) compatible and complete attribute structure corresponding to it. Based on these foundations, we now proceed to generate polytomous states and structures using these attribute maps and structures within a disjunctive framework.

This generation mechanism preserves the core disjunctive principle of dichotomous KST – where mastery of at least one required skill determines item success – but generalizes it through an item-response function that selects the maximal attainable value when multiple response thresholds meet the disjunctive condition.

                               p 
                             to the same polytomous state  
                               
                                  { 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     p 
                                     , 
                                     
                                        
                                           v 
                                        
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                     
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  , 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     q 
                                     , 
                                     
                                        
                                           v 
                                        
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                     
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  } 
                               
                            , forming an equivalence class under  
                               p 
                            . This demonstrates that  
                               p 
                             is not injective in this example, indicating that the mapping between the attribute structure and the polytomous assessment structure is not necessarily one-to-one. The corresponding polytomous assessment structure  
                               K 
                             is depicted in  Fig. 1(b) .

If the set of response values  
                         V 
                       forms a finite complete lattice, a polytomous assessment structure can exhibit a richer internal organization. Given two mappings  
                         
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                            ∈ 
                            K 
                         
                      , their  supremum  is defined as the mapping  
                         
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            ⊔ 
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                            : 
                            Q 
                            → 
                            L 
                         
                      , where for each  
                         
                            q 
                            ∈ 
                            Q 
                         
                      ,  
                         
                            
                               
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     
                                        
                                           K 
                                        
                                        
                                           1 
                                        
                                     
                                     ⊔ 
                                     
                                        
                                           K 
                                        
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                     
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     q 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  = 
                                  sup 
                                  
                                     { 
                                     
                                        
                                           K 
                                        
                                        
                                           1 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        q 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     , 
                                     
                                        
                                           K 
                                        
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        q 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                     } 
                                  
                                  . 
                               
                            
                         
                      A polytomous assessment structure  
                         K 
                       is said to be  closed under the supremum  if, for any  
                         
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                            ∈ 
                            K 
                         
                      , their supremum  
                         
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            ⊔ 
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                         
                       also belongs to  
                         K 
                       ( Stefanutti, Anselmi et al., 2020 ).

                         p 
                       be the disjunctive item-response function associated with  
                         
                            ( 
                            R

                            A

                            )

                            p

                               q

                            p

                               q

                                     v 
                                  
                                  
                                     q 
                                  
                               
                               } 
                            
                         
                       for all  
                         
                            q 
                            ∈ 
                            Q 
                         
                      . For convenience, we denote this as  
                         
                            p

                               C 
                               )

                               q

Remark 3.2 
                         If the response value set  
                               V 
                             in  Theorem 3.5  is replaced by a finite linear order, then any subset  
                               
                                  C 
                                  ⊆ 
                                  A 
                               
                             is  
                               d 
                            -compatible. Consequently, the maximal attribute structure  
                               C 
                             is given by  
                               
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                                  
                                     A 
                                  
                               
                            , and the corresponding polytomous assessment structure  
                               
                                  p 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     C 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             is closed under the supremum.

If the response value set  
                               V 
                             in  Theorem 3.5  is replaced by a finite linear order, then any subset  
                               
                                  C 
                                  ⊆ 
                                  A 
                               
                             is  
                               d 
                            -compatible. Consequently, the maximal attribute structure  
                               C 
                             is given by

                                     A 
                                  
                               
                            , and the corresponding polytomous assessment structure  
                               
                                  p 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     C 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             is closed under the supremum.

In a calculus course, continuity, derivatives, and differentiability are three key analytical properties of functions. For a one-variable function  
                         
                            y 
                            =

                      , is depicted in  Fig. 3(b) .

This section analyzes the fundamental divergence between conjunctive and disjunctive attribute maps in polytomous KST. In classical dichotomous models, skill maps may admit both conjunctive and disjunctive interpretations; however, when KST is extended to the polytomous case, the requirements imposed by the two approaches become mutually exclusive. In both models, Condition (C2) for conjunctive maps and Condition (D2) for disjunctive maps are introduced to ensure that lower-level response values remain observable through the item-response function, yet they achieve this via opposing mechanisms.

The conjunctive approach can lead to upward attribute accumulation (e.g., if

                               v 
                            
                         ). In both frameworks, Condition (C2) and Condition (D2) serve to keep lower-level responses observable through their respective item-response functions, yet they implement this goal via fundamentally opposed rules—a conflict that becomes irreconcilable in non-linear partial orders.

From a practical perspective, this theoretical split helps guide model selection. Conjunctive models work best in domains with cumulative skill hierarchies (e.g., settings where more advanced abilities build on simpler ones, such as language learning). Disjunctive models, on the other hand, are well-suited for domains with relatively independent skills (e.g., multilingual translation, where different attributes trigger different responses). Hybrid attempts to satisfy both constraints typically fail, except in trivial cases such as linear orders with singleton attribute sets.

In dichotomous KST, the same skill map can be used to generate knowledge structures in either a disjunctive or conjunctive model, depending on the context, and these two structures are often duals of each other (e.g.,  Falmagne & Doignon, 2011 ). As elaborated in Section  5 , the extension of these principles to polytomous KST reveals important distinctions between conjunctive and disjunctive attribute maps. Specifically, the conjunctive attribute map proposed by  Stefanutti et al. (2023) , constrained by Condition (C2), yields a special structure where  
                         
                            τ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                            ⊂ 
                            τ 
                            
                               ( 
                               q 
                               , 
                               w 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       for  
                         
                            v 
                            ≺ 
                            w 
                         
                      . Similarly, the disjunctive attribute map  
                         μ 
                       presented in this paper satisfies Condition (D2) but cannot be applied to the conjunctive model. These differences highlight that attribute maps in polytomous KST are not universally interchangeable across models, unlike in classical dichotomous KST.

Given that  Stefanutti et al. (2023)  established a solid paradigm for the conjunctive model in the polytomous context, it is equally important to examine the compatibility and completeness of attribute structures under the disjunctive model (see  Definitions 3.2–3.3 ). These considerations ensure the validity of the polytomous states generated by the disjunctive item–response function (see Eq.  (1) ) and the coverage of the polytomous assessment structure (i.e., all admissible pairs can be observed) (see  Theorems 3.3–3.4 ).

It is essential to provide more detailed explanations for  Theorems 3.1 and 3.2 .  Theorem 3.1  establishes that, in the disjunctive sense, a valid attribute structure can exist in such a way that every effective attribute forms a singleton set. These singleton sets then become fundamental building blocks for constructing the attribute structure.

Theorem 3.2  further characterizes certain features of (maximal) attribute structures under the disjunctive model, providing a means to identify or construct appropriate attribute structures. It can be compared to Theorem 2 from  Stefanutti et al. (2023) . In the conjunctive model,  Stefanutti et al. (2023)  demonstrated that if there exists a maximal attribute subset  
                         C 
                       that is compatible with the conjunctive attribute map  
                         τ 
                      , then the power set  
                         
                            
                               2 
                            
                            
                               C 
                            
                         
                       is also compatible with  
                         τ 
                      . By identifying all such maximal subsets  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               ′ 
                            
                         
                      , the maximal compatible attribute structure can be determined within the conjunctive model.

In contrast, within the disjunctive model, the goal is to identify a maximal subset  
                         B 
                       such that every two-element subset within  
                         B 
                       remains compatible with the disjunctive attribute map  
                         μ 
                      . Consequently, the power set  
                         
                            
                               2 
                            
                            
                               B 
                            
                         
                       is compatible with  
                         μ 
                      . By identifying all such maximal subsets  
                         
                            
                               B 
                            
                            
                               ′ 
                            
                         
                      , we can derive the maximal compatible attribute structure in the disjunctive context. This approach is precisely how the maximal compatible attribute structure was identified in  Example 3.3  and the example provided in Section  4 .

Beyond its theoretical significance, the example in Section  4  illustrates how the disjunctive model can be applied to understanding diverse learning pathways in mathematical concept acquisition. The attribute assignments in  Table 2  capture different cognitive strategies—some students apply definitions directly (e.g.,

                               1 
                            
                         
                       for continuity), while others infer relationships (e.g.,

                       for continuity via differentiability). The disjunctive attribute map further reveals multiple solution pathways. For example, a student reaching  
                         
                            
                               v 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                       for  
                         
                            
                               q 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       (partial derivatives) may do so through direct computation ( 
                         
                            
                               a 
                            
                            
                               3 
                            
                         
                      ) or continuity-based inference ( 
                         
                            
                               a 
                            
                            
                               4 
                            
                         
                      ). Recognizing these alternative approaches enables more personalized instruction and adaptive assessment.

As knowledge assessments increasingly adopt polytomous response formats, this model offers a flexible framework for tracking student progress across incremental competence levels. By mapping diverse reasoning strategies, it supports targeted feedback and enhances conceptual mastery in subjects like calculus, making it applicable to educational platforms and adaptive learning environments.

During the past two decades, CbKST has seen significant advancements, especially in supporting personalized learning and knowledge evaluation (e.g.,  Anselmi, Robusto, & Stefanutti, 2012; Anselmi, Stefanutti, de Chiusole, & Robusto, 2017; Heller, Steiner, Hockemeyer, & Albert, 2006; Spoto, Stefanutti, & Vidotto, 2016; Xie, Xu, & Li, 2023 ). The findings by  Stefanutti et al. (2023)  represent a pivotal extension of these classical results into the polytomous context. This paper aimed to fill the existing gap by introducing the disjunctive model within this polytomous framework. Building upon the foundations of both the conjunctive and disjunctive models, future research is anticipated to extend competence models further and yield richer results for CbKST in the polytomous context.
